**Afghanistan**,[^5] officially the **Islamic Emirate of Afghanistan**,[^6] is a [landlocked country](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Landlocked_country "Landlocked country") located at the crossroads of [Central](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Central_Asia "Central Asia") and [South Asia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_Asia "South Asia"). It is bordered by [Pakistan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pakistan "Pakistan") to the [east and south](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Durand_Line "Durand Line"),[^7] [Iran](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran "Iran") to the [west](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afghanistan%E2%80%93Iran_border "Afghanistan–Iran border"), [Turkmenistan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Turkmenistan "Turkmenistan") to the [northwest](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afghanistan%E2%80%93Turkmenistan_border "Afghanistan–Turkmenistan border"), [Uzbekistan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Uzbekistan "Uzbekistan") to the [north](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afghanistan%E2%80%93Uzbekistan_border "Afghanistan–Uzbekistan border"), [Tajikistan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tajikistan "Tajikistan") to the [northeast](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afghanistan%E2%80%93Tajikistan_border "Afghanistan–Tajikistan border"), and [China](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/China "China") to the [northeast and east](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afghanistan%E2%80%93China_border "Afghanistan–China border"). Occupying 652,864 square kilometers (252,072 sq mi) of land, the country is predominantly mountainous with plains [in the north](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afghan_Turkestan "Afghan Turkestan") and [the southwest](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sistan_Basin "Sistan Basin"), which are separated by the [Hindu Kush](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hindu_Kush "Hindu Kush") mountain range. [Kabul](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kabul "Kabul") is the country's capital and largest city. [Afghanistan's population](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Demographics_of_Afghanistan "Demographics of Afghanistan") is estimated to be between 35 and 50 million.[^4]

[Human habitation in Afghanistan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ancient_history_of_Afghanistan "Ancient history of Afghanistan") dates to the [Middle Paleolithic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Middle_Paleolithic "Middle Paleolithic") era. Popularly referred to as the [graveyard of empires](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graveyard_of_empires "Graveyard of empires"),[^43] the land [has witnessed numerous military campaigns](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Invasions_of_Afghanistan "Invasions of Afghanistan"), including those by the [Persians](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Persian_empire "Persian empire"), [Alexander the Great](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_the_Great "Alexander the Great"), the [Maurya Empire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maurya_Empire "Maurya Empire"), [Arab Muslims](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Muslim_conquests_of_Afghanistan "Muslim conquests of Afghanistan"), the [Mongols](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mongol_invasion_of_the_Khwarazmian_Empire "Mongol invasion of the Khwarazmian Empire"), the [British](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_influence_in_Afghanistan#The_Great_Game "European influence in Afghanistan"), the [Soviet Union](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet%E2%80%93Afghan_War "Soviet–Afghan War"), and [a US-led coalition](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/War_in_Afghanistan_\(2001%E2%80%932021\) "War in Afghanistan (2001–2021)"). Afghanistan also served as the source from which the [Greco-Bactrians](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greco-Bactrian_Kingdom "Greco-Bactrian Kingdom") and the [Mughals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mughal_Empire "Mughal Empire"), among others, rose to form major empires.[^44] Because of the various conquests and periods in both the [Iranian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greater_Iran "Greater Iran") and [Indian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greater_India "Greater India") cultural spheres,[^45] [^46] the area was a center for [Zoroastrianism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zoroastrianism "Zoroastrianism"), [Buddhism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Buddhism "Buddhism"), [Hinduism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hinduism "Hinduism"), and later [Islam](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Islam "Islam").[^47] The modern state of Afghanistan began with the [Durrani Empire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Durrani_Empire "Durrani Empire") in the 18th century under [Ahmad Shah Durrani](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ahmad_Shah_Durrani "Ahmad Shah Durrani"),[^48] although [Dost Mohammad Khan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dost_Mohammad_Khan "Dost Mohammad Khan") is sometimes considered to be the founder of the first [modern Afghan state](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Emirate_of_Afghanistan "Emirate of Afghanistan").[^49] Afghanistan became a [buffer state](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Buffer_state "Buffer state") in the [Great Game](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_Game "Great Game") between the [British Empire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/British_Empire "British Empire") and the [Russian Empire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russian_Empire "Russian Empire"). From [India](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/British_Raj "British Raj"), the British attempted to subjugate Afghanistan but were repelled in the [First Anglo-Afghan War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/First_Anglo-Afghan_War "First Anglo-Afghan War"); the [Second Anglo-Afghan War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Second_Anglo-Afghan_War "Second Anglo-Afghan War") saw a British victory. Following the [Third Anglo-Afghan War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Third_Anglo-Afghan_War "Third Anglo-Afghan War") in 1919, Afghanistan became free of foreign political hegemony, and emerged as the independent [Kingdom of Afghanistan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kingdom_of_Afghanistan "Kingdom of Afghanistan") in 1926; this monarchy lasted almost half a century.

Since the late 1970s, Afghanistan's history has been dominated by extensive warfare, including [coups, invasions, insurgencies, and civil wars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afghan_conflict "Afghan conflict"). This period of instability began in 1973 when King [Zahir Shah](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mohammed_Zahir_Shah "Mohammed Zahir Shah") was [overthrown](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1973_Afghan_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat "1973 Afghan coup d'état") by his cousin [Mohammad Daoud Khan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mohammad_Daoud_Khan "Mohammad Daoud Khan"), ending the monarchy and resulting in the establishment of the [Republic of Afghanistan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Republic_of_Afghanistan_\(1973%E2%80%931978\) "Republic of Afghanistan (1973–1978)"). Daoud Khan became the [President of Afghanistan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/President_of_Afghanistan "President of Afghanistan"), ruling as an [autocrat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Autocracy "Autocracy"). In 1978 the conflict became a violent one when [a communist revolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saur_Revolution "Saur Revolution") overthrew and assassinated Daoud Khan, establishing the socialist [Democratic Republic of Afghanistan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_Afghanistan "Democratic Republic of Afghanistan"). Subsequent infighting prompted the [Soviet Union to invade Afghanistan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_invasion_of_Afghanistan "Soviet invasion of Afghanistan") in 1979.

The [*mujahideen*](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afghan_mujahideen "Afghan mujahideen") won against the Soviets in the [Soviet–Afghan War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet%E2%80%93Afghan_War "Soviet–Afghan War"), leading to the [Soviets' withdrawal in 1989](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_withdrawal_from_Afghanistan "Soviet withdrawal from Afghanistan"). The ensuing [civil war](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afghan_Civil_War_\(1989%E2%80%931992\) "Afghan Civil War (1989–1992)") overthrew the socialist republic and established the [Islamic State of Afghanistan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Islamic_State_of_Afghanistan "Islamic State of Afghanistan") through the [Peshawar Accord](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peshawar_Accord "Peshawar Accord"), which could not remain in force and resulted in a [second civil war](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afghan_Civil_War_\(1992%E2%80%931996\) "Afghan Civil War (1992–1996)") between *mujahideen* factions in 1992. The [Taliban](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taliban "Taliban") controlled most of the country by 1996, but their [Islamic Emirate of Afghanistan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Islamic_Emirate_of_Afghanistan_\(1996%E2%80%932001\) "Islamic Emirate of Afghanistan (1996–2001)") received little international recognition before its overthrow in the 2001 [US invasion of Afghanistan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_invasion_of_Afghanistan "United States invasion of Afghanistan"). The Taliban returned to power in 2021 after [capturing Kabul](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fall_of_Kabul_\(2021\) "Fall of Kabul (2021)"), ending the [2001–2021 war](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/War_in_Afghanistan_\(2001%E2%80%932021\) "War in Afghanistan (2001–2021)"). The Taliban government is [widely unrecognized](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Recognition_of_the_Islamic_Emirate_of_Afghanistan "Recognition of the Islamic Emirate of Afghanistan") [^8] by the international community due to reported violations of [human rights in Afghanistan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_rights_in_Afghanistan "Human rights in Afghanistan"), particularly regarding the [treatment of women by the Taliban](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Treatment_of_women_by_the_Taliban "Treatment of women by the Taliban").

Afghanistan is rich in natural resources, including [lithium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lithium "Lithium"), iron, [zinc](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zinc "Zinc"), and copper. It is the third largest producer of both [saffron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saffron "Saffron") [^51] and [cashmere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cashmere_wool "Cashmere wool").[^52] The country is a member of the [South Asian Association for Regional Cooperation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_Asian_Association_for_Regional_Cooperation "South Asian Association for Regional Cooperation") and a founding member of the [Organization of Islamic Cooperation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Organization_of_Islamic_Cooperation "Organization of Islamic Cooperation"). Due to the effects of war in recent decades, the country has dealt with high levels of terrorism, [poverty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Poverty_in_Afghanistan "Poverty in Afghanistan"), and child malnutrition. Afghanistan remains among the world's [least developed countries](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Least_developed_country "Least developed country") with [its economic output](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Economy_of_Afghanistan "Economy of Afghanistan") per capita among the [lowest of any country](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP_\(nominal\)_per_capita "List of countries by GDP (nominal) per capita").

## Etymology

Some scholars suggest that the [root](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Root_\(linguistics\) "Root (linguistics)") name [*Afghān*](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afghan_\(ethnonym\) "Afghan (ethnonym)") is derived from the [Sanskrit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sanskrit "Sanskrit") word *[Aśvakan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/A%C5%9Bvaka "Aśvaka")*, which was the name used for ancient inhabitants of the [Hindu Kush](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hindu_Kush "Hindu Kush").[^53] *Aśvakan* literally means "horsemen", "horse breeders", or " [cavalrymen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cavalry "Cavalry") " (from *[aśva](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/A%C5%9Bva "Aśva")*, the [Sanskrit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sanskrit "Sanskrit") and [Avestan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Avestan "Avestan") words for "horse").[^54]

Historically, the ethnonym *Afghān* was used to refer to ethnic [Pashtuns](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pashtuns "Pashtuns").[^55] The Arabic and Persian form of the name, *Afġān*, was first attested in the 10th-century geography book *[Hudud al-'Alam](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hudud_al-%27Alam "Hudud al-'Alam")*.[^56] The last part of the name, " *[\-stan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/-stan "-stan")* ", is a Persian suffix meaning "place of". Therefore, "Afghanistan" translates to "land of the Afghans", or "land of the Pashtuns" in a historical sense. According to the third edition of the *[Encyclopaedia of Islam](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Encyclopaedia_of_Islam "Encyclopaedia of Islam")*:[^57]

> The name Afghanistan (Afghānistān, land of the Afghans / Pashtuns, *afāghina*, sing. *afghān*) can be traced to the early eighth/fourteenth century, when it designated the easternmost part of the [Kartid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kartid "Kartid") realm. This name was later used for certain regions in the [Ṣafavid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Safavid_Iran "Safavid Iran") and [Mughal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mughal_Empire "Mughal Empire") empires that were inhabited by Afghans. While based on a state-supporting elite of [Abdālī / Durrānī](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Durrani "Durrani") Afghans, the [Sadūzāʾī Durrānī polity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Durrani_Empire "Durrani Empire") that came into being in 1160 / 1747 was not called Afghanistan in its own day. The name became a state designation only during the colonial intervention of the nineteenth century.

The term "Afghanistan" was officially used in 1855, when the British recognized [Dost Mohammad Khan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dost_Mohammad_Khan "Dost Mohammad Khan") as king of [Afghanistan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Emirate_of_Afghanistan "Emirate of Afghanistan").[^58]

## History

### Prehistory and antiquity

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Nomads_in_Badghis_Province.jpg/250px-Nomads_in_Badghis_Province.jpg)

Tents of Afghan nomads in the northern Badghis Province. Early peasant farming villages came into existence about 7,000 years ago.

Excavations of prehistoric sites suggest that humans were living in what is now Afghanistan at least 50,000 years ago, and that farming communities in the area were among the earliest in the world. An important site of early historical activities, many believe that Afghanistan compares to [Egypt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Egypt "Egypt") in the historical value of its archaeological sites.[^59] [^60] Artifacts typical of the [Paleolithic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paleolithic "Paleolithic"), [Mesolithic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mesolithic "Mesolithic"), [Neolithic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neolithic "Neolithic"), [Bronze](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bronze_Age "Bronze Age"), and [Iron Ages](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iron_Age "Iron Age") have been found in Afghanistan. Urban civilization is believed to have begun as early as 3000 BCE, and the early city of [Mundigak](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mundigak "Mundigak") (near [Kandahar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kandahar "Kandahar") in the south of the country) was a center of the [Helmand culture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Helmand_culture "Helmand culture"). More recent findings established that the [Indus Valley Civilization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indus_Valley_Civilization "Indus Valley Civilization") stretched up towards modern-day Afghanistan. An Indus Valley site has been found on the [Oxus River](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oxus_River "Oxus River") at [Shortugai](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shortugai "Shortugai") in northern Afghanistan.[^61] [^62] [^63]

After 2000 BCE successive waves of semi-nomadic people from Central Asia began moving south into Afghanistan; among them were many [Indo-European](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indo-European_languages "Indo-European languages") -speaking [Indo-Iranians](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indo-Iranians "Indo-Iranians"). These tribes later migrated further into South Asia, Western Asia, and toward Europe via the area north of the [Caspian Sea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Caspian_Sea "Caspian Sea"). The region at the time was referred to as [Ariana](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ariana "Ariana").[^59] [^64] By the middle of the 6th century BCE, the Achaemenids overthrew the [Medes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Medes "Medes") and incorporated [Arachosia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arachosia "Arachosia"), [Aria](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aria_\(satrapy\) "Aria (satrapy)"), and [Bactria](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bactria "Bactria") within its eastern boundaries. An [inscription](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Epigraphy "Epigraphy") on the tombstone of [Darius I of Persia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Darius_I_of_Persia "Darius I of Persia") mentions the [Kabul Valley](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kabulistan "Kabulistan") in a list of the 29 countries that he had conquered.[^65] The region of [Arachosia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arachosia "Arachosia"), around Kandahar in modern-day southern Afghanistan, used to be primarily Zoroastrian and played a key role in the transfer of the Avesta to [Persia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Persis "Persis") and is thus considered by some to be the "second homeland of Zoroastrianism".[^66] [^67] [^68]

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/ScythianBelt.jpg/250px-ScythianBelt.jpg)

A "Bactrian gold" Scythian belt depicting Dionysus, from Tillya Tepe in the ancient region of Bactria

[Alexander the Great](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_the_Great "Alexander the Great") and his Macedonian forces arrived in Afghanistan in 330 BCE after defeating [Darius III of Persia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Darius_III_of_Persia "Darius III of Persia") a year earlier in the [Battle of Gaugamela](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Battle_of_Gaugamela "Battle of Gaugamela"). Following Alexander's brief occupation, the successor state of the [Seleucid Empire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seleucid_Empire "Seleucid Empire") controlled the region until 305 BCE, when they gave much of it to the [Maurya Empire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maurya_Empire "Maurya Empire") as part of an alliance treaty. The Mauryans controlled the area south of the [Hindu Kush](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hindu_Kush "Hindu Kush") until they were overthrown in about 185 BCE. Their decline began 60 years after [Ashoka](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ashoka "Ashoka") 's rule ended, leading to the [Hellenistic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hellenistic "Hellenistic") reconquest by the [Greco-Bactrians](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greco-Bactrians "Greco-Bactrians"). Much of it soon broke away and became part of the [Indo-Greek Kingdom](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indo-Greek_Kingdom "Indo-Greek Kingdom"). They were defeated and expelled by the [Indo-Scythians](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indo-Scythians "Indo-Scythians") in the late 2nd century BCE.[^69] [^70] The [Silk Road](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Silk_Road "Silk Road") appeared during the first century BCE, and Afghanistan flourished with trade, with routes to China, India, Persia, and north to the cities of [Bukhara](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bukhara "Bukhara"), [Samarkand](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Samarkand "Samarkand"), and [Khiva](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Khiva "Khiva") in present-day Uzbekistan.[^71] Goods and ideas were exchanged at this center point, such as Chinese silk, Persian silver and Roman gold, while the region of present Afghanistan was mining and trading [lapis lazuli](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lapis_lazuli "Lapis lazuli") stones [^72] mainly from the [Badakhshan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Badakhshan "Badakhshan") region.

During the first century BCE, the [Parthian Empire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parthian_Empire "Parthian Empire") subjugated the region but lost it to their [Indo-Parthian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indo-Parthian "Indo-Parthian") vassals. In the mid-to-late first century CE the vast [Kushan Empire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kushan_Empire "Kushan Empire"), centered in Afghanistan, became great patrons of Buddhist culture, making [Buddhism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Buddhism "Buddhism") flourish throughout the region. The Kushans were overthrown by the [Sassanids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sassanids "Sassanids") in the 3rd century CE, though the [Indo-Sassanids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indo-Sassanids "Indo-Sassanids") continued to rule at least parts of the region. They were followed by the [Kidarites](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kidarite "Kidarite") who, in turn, was replaced by the [Hephthalites](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hephthalites "Hephthalites"). They were replaced by the [Turk Shahi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Turk_Shahi "Turk Shahi") in the 7th century. The Buddhist Turk Shahi of Kabul was replaced by a Hindu dynasty before the Saffarids conquered the area in 870; this Hindu dynasty was called [Hindu Shahi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hindu_Shahi "Hindu Shahi").[^73] Much of the northeastern and southern areas of the country remained dominated by [Buddhist](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Buddhist "Buddhist") culture.[^74] [^75]

### Medieval period

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Ghurid_Empire_according_to_Schwartzberg_Atlas%2C_p.147.png/250px-Ghurid_Empire_according_to_Schwartzberg_Atlas%2C_p.147.png)

The Ghurids originated from Ghor Province in central Afghanistan.

[Arab](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arab "Arab") [Muslims](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Muslim "Muslim") brought Islam to [Herat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Herat "Herat") and [Zaranj](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zaranj "Zaranj") in 642 CE and began spreading eastward; some of the native inhabitants they encountered accepted it while others revolted. Before the [arrival of Islam](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Islamic_conquest_of_Afghanistan "Islamic conquest of Afghanistan"), the region used to be home to various beliefs and cults, often resulting in [Syncretism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Syncretism "Syncretism") between the dominant religions [^76] [^77] such as [Zoroastrianism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zoroastrianism "Zoroastrianism"),[^66] [^67] [^68] Buddhism or [Greco-Buddhism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greco-Buddhism "Greco-Buddhism"), [Ancient Iranian religions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ancient_Iranian_religion "Ancient Iranian religion"),[^78] [Hinduism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hinduism "Hinduism"), Christianity,[^79] [^80] and Judaism.[^81] [^82] An exemplification of the syncretism in the region would be that people were patrons of Buddhism but still worshipped local Iranian gods such as [Ahura Mazda](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ahura_Mazda "Ahura Mazda"), [Lady Nana](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nana_\(Kushan_goddess\) "Nana (Kushan goddess)"), [Anahita](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anahita "Anahita") or [Mihr (Mithra)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mithra "Mithra") and portrayed [Greek gods](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greek_mythology "Greek mythology") as protectors of Buddha.[^83] [^78] [^84] The [Zunbils](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zunbils "Zunbils") and Kabul Shahi were first conquered in 870 CE by the [Saffarid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saffarid_dynasty "Saffarid dynasty") Muslims of Zaranj. Later, the [Samanids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Samanids "Samanids") extended their Islamic influence south of the Hindu Kush. The [Ghaznavids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ghaznavids "Ghaznavids") rose to power in the 10th century.[^85] [^86] [^87]

By the 11th century, [Mahmud of Ghazni](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mahmud_of_Ghazni "Mahmud of Ghazni") had defeated the remaining Hindu rulers and effectively [Islamized](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Islamized "Islamized") the wider region,[^88] with the exception of [Kafiristan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kafiristan "Kafiristan").[^89] Mahmud made [Ghazni](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ghazni "Ghazni") into an important city and patronized intellectuals such as the historian [al-Biruni](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Al-Biruni "Al-Biruni") and the poet [Ferdowsi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ferdowsi "Ferdowsi").[^90] The [Ghaznavid dynasty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ghaznavid_dynasty "Ghaznavid dynasty") was [overthrown by the Ghurids in 1186](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Siege_of_Lahore_\(1186\) "Siege of Lahore (1186)"), whose architectural achievements included the remote [Minaret of Jam](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Minaret_of_Jam "Minaret of Jam"). The Ghurids controlled Afghanistan for less than a century before being conquered by the [Khwarazmian dynasty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Khwarazmian_dynasty "Khwarazmian dynasty") in 1215.[^91]

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Genghis_Khan_empire-en.svg/330px-Genghis_Khan_empire-en.svg.png)

Mongol invasions and conquests

In 1219 CE, [Genghis Khan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Genghis_Khan "Genghis Khan") and his [Mongol army overran the region](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mongol_invasion_of_the_Khwarazmian_Empire "Mongol invasion of the Khwarazmian Empire"). His troops are said to have annihilated the Khwarazmian cities of Herat and [Balkh](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Balkh "Balkh") as well as [Bamyan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Siege_of_Bamyan "Siege of Bamyan").[^92] The destruction caused by the Mongols forced many locals to return to an agrarian rural society.[^93] Mongol rule continued with the [Ilkhanate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ilkhanate "Ilkhanate") in the northwest while the [Khalji dynasty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Khalji_dynasty "Khalji dynasty") administered the Afghan tribal areas south of the Hindu Kush until the invasion of [Timur](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Timur "Timur") (aka Tamerlane), who established the [Timurid Empire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Timurid_Empire "Timurid Empire") in 1370. Under the rule of [Shah Rukh](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shah_Rukh "Shah Rukh"), the city of Herat [^94] served as the focal point of the [Timurid Renaissance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Timurid_Renaissance "Timurid Renaissance"), whose glory matched [Florence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Florence "Florence") of the [Italian Renaissance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Italian_Renaissance "Italian Renaissance") as the center of a cultural rebirth.[^95] [^96]

In the early 16th century [Babur](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Babur "Babur") arrived from [Ferghana](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ferghana "Ferghana") and captured Kabul from the [Arghun dynasty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arghun_dynasty "Arghun dynasty").[^97] Babur would go on to conquer the Afghan [Lodi dynasty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lodi_dynasty "Lodi dynasty") who had ruled the Delhi Sultanate in the [First Battle of Panipat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/First_Battle_of_Panipat "First Battle of Panipat").[^98] Between the 16th and 18th century, the Uzbek [Khanate of Bukhara](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Khanate_of_Bukhara "Khanate of Bukhara"), Iranian [Safavids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Safavids "Safavids"), and Indian [Mughals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mughals "Mughals") ruled parts of the territory.[^99] During the medieval period, the northwestern area of Afghanistan was referred to by the regional name [Khorasan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greater_Khorasan "Greater Khorasan"), which was commonly used up to the 19th century among natives to describe their country.[^100] [^101] [^102] [^103]

### Hotak dynasty

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Mapofthehotaks1728.png/330px-Mapofthehotaks1728.png)

Map of the Hotak Empire at its height in 1728, disputed between Hussain Hotak (centered in Kandahar) and Ashraf Hotak (centered in Isfahan)

In 1709, [Mirwais Hotak](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mirwais_Hotak "Mirwais Hotak"), a local [Ghilzai](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ghilzai "Ghilzai") tribal leader, successfully rebelled against the Safavids. He defeated [Gurgin Khan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gurgin_Khan "Gurgin Khan"), the Georgian governor of Kandahar under the Safavids, and established his own kingdom.[^104] Mirwais died in 1715, and was succeeded by his brother [Abdul Aziz](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abdul_Aziz_Hotak "Abdul Aziz Hotak"), who was soon killed by Mirwais's son [Mahmud](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mahmud_Hotak "Mahmud Hotak") for possibly planning to sign a peace with the Safavids. Mahmud led the Afghan army in 1722 to the Persian capital of [Isfahan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isfahan "Isfahan"), and captured the city after the [Battle of Gulnabad](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Battle_of_Gulnabad "Battle of Gulnabad") and proclaimed himself King of Persia.[^104] The Afghan dynasty was ousted from Persia by [Nader Shah](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nader_Shah "Nader Shah") after the 1729 [Battle of Damghan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Battle_of_Damghan_\(1729\) "Battle of Damghan (1729)").

In 1738, Nader Shah and his [forces](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afsharid_dynasty "Afsharid dynasty") captured Kandahar in the [siege of Kandahar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Siege_of_Kandahar "Siege of Kandahar"), the last Hotak stronghold, from Shah [Hussain Hotak](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hussain_Hotak "Hussain Hotak"). Soon after, the Persian and Afghan forces [invaded India](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nader_Shah%27s_invasion_of_India "Nader Shah's invasion of India"), Nader Shah had plundered Delhi, alongside his 16-year-old commander, [Ahmad Shah Durrani](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ahmad_Shah_Durrani "Ahmad Shah Durrani") who had assisted him on these campaigns. Nader Shah was assassinated in 1747.[^105] [^106]

### Durrani Empire

Ahmad Shah had launched two campaigns into Khorasan, 1750–1751 and 1754–1755.[^107]

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Portrait_of_Ahmad-Shah_Durrani._Mughal_miniature._ca._1757%2C_Biblioth%C3%A8que_nationale_de_France.jpg/250px-Portrait_of_Ahmad-Shah_Durrani._Mughal_miniature._ca._1757%2C_Biblioth%C3%A8que_nationale_de_France.jpg)

Portrait of Ahmad Shah Durrani, c. 1757

### Barakzai dynasty and British wars

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Map_of_Afghanistan_March_1839.png/330px-Map_of_Afghanistan_March_1839.png)

Map of Afghanistan ( Emirate ) and surrounding nations in 1839, during the First Anglo-Afghan War. Dost Mohammad Khan 's realm can be seen as the Emirate of Kabul, with the Principality of Qandahar and the Emirate of Herat seen as well.

By the early 19th century, the Afghan empire was under threat from the [Persians](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Qajar_dynasty "Qajar dynasty") in the west and the [Sikh Empire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sikh_Empire "Sikh Empire") in the east. [Fateh Khan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fateh_Khan_Barakzai "Fateh Khan Barakzai"), leader of the [Barakzai tribe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barakzai_dynasty "Barakzai dynasty"), installed many of his brothers in positions of power throughout the empire. Fateh Khan was [brutally murdered](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Torture_and_Execution_of_Fateh_Khan_Barakzai "Torture and Execution of Fateh Khan Barakzai") in 1818 by [Mahmud Shah](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mahmud_Shah_Durrani "Mahmud Shah Durrani"). As a result, the brothers of Fateh Khan and the [Barakzai](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barakzai "Barakzai") tribe rebelled, and a civil war brewed. During this turbulent period, Afghanistan fractured into many states, including the [Principality of Qandahar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Principality_of_Qandahar "Principality of Qandahar"), [Emirate of Herat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Herat_\(1793%E2%80%931863\) "Herat (1793–1863)"), [Khanate of Qunduz](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kunduz_Khanate "Kunduz Khanate"), [Maimana Khanate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maimana_Khanate "Maimana Khanate"), and numerous other warring polities. The most prominent state was the [Emirate of Kabul](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Emirate_of_Afghanistan "Emirate of Afghanistan"), ruled by [Dost Mohammad Khan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dost_Mohammad_Khan "Dost Mohammad Khan").[^108] [^109]

[Akbar Khan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wazir_Akbar_Khan "Wazir Akbar Khan") and the Afghan army failed to capture the [Jamrud Fort](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jamrud_Fort "Jamrud Fort") from the [Sikh Khalsa Army](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sikh_Khalsa_Army "Sikh Khalsa Army"), but killed Sikh commander [Hari Singh Nalwa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hari_Singh_Nalwa "Hari Singh Nalwa"), thus ending the [Afghan-Sikh Wars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afghan-Sikh_Wars "Afghan-Sikh Wars").[^110]

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Afghan_foot_soldiers_in_1841.jpg/250px-Afghan_foot_soldiers_in_1841.jpg)

Afghan tribesmen in 1841, painted by British officer James Rattray

In 1893, Abdur Rahman signed an agreement in which the ethnic Pashtun and [Baloch](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Baloch_people "Baloch people") territories were divided by the [Durand Line](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Durand_Line "Durand Line"), which forms the modern-day border between Pakistan and Afghanistan. [Shia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia_Islam_in_Afghanistan "Shia Islam in Afghanistan") -dominated [Hazarajat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hazarajat "Hazarajat") and pagan [Kafiristan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kafiristan "Kafiristan") remained politically independent until being [conquered](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Muslim_conquests_of_Afghanistan "Muslim conquests of Afghanistan") by Abdur Rahman Khan in 1891–1896. He was known as the "Iron Amir" for his features and his ruthless methods against tribes.[^111] He died in 1901, succeeded by his son, [Habibullah Khan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Habibullah_Khan "Habibullah Khan").

> How can a small power like Afghanistan, which is like a goat between these lions \[Britain and Russia\] or a grain of wheat between two strong millstones of the grinding mill, stand in the midway of the stones without being ground to dust?

—  [Abdur Rahman Khan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abdur_Rahman_Khan "Abdur Rahman Khan"), the "Iron Amir", in 1900 [^112] [^113]

During the [First World War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/First_World_War "First World War"), when Afghanistan was neutral, Habibullah Khan was met by officials of the [Central Powers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Central_Powers "Central Powers") in the [Niedermayer–Hentig Expedition](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Niedermayer%E2%80%93Hentig_Expedition "Niedermayer–Hentig Expedition"). They called on Afghanistan to declare full independence from the United Kingdom, join them and attack British India, as part of the [Hindu–German Conspiracy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hindu%E2%80%93German_Conspiracy "Hindu–German Conspiracy"). The effort to bring Afghanistan into the Central Powers failed, but it sparked discontent among the population about maintaining neutrality with the British. Habibullah was assassinated in February 1919, and [Amanullah Khan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amanullah_Khan "Amanullah Khan") eventually assumed power. A staunch supporter of the 1915–1916 expeditions, Amanullah Khan invaded British India, beginning the [Third Anglo-Afghan War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Third_Anglo-Afghan_War "Third Anglo-Afghan War"), and entering British India via the [Khyber Pass](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Khyber_Pass "Khyber Pass").[^114]

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/King_Amanullah_standing.jpg/250px-King_Amanullah_standing.jpg)

Amanullah Khan proclaimed himself King of Afghanistan in June 1926.

Following the end of the Third Anglo-Afghan War and the signing of the [Treaty of Rawalpindi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anglo-Afghan_Treaty_of_1919 "Anglo-Afghan Treaty of 1919") on 19 August 1919, [Emir](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Emir_of_Afghanistan "Emir of Afghanistan") Amanullah Khan declared the [Emirate of Afghanistan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Emirate_of_Afghanistan "Emirate of Afghanistan") a [sovereign](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sovereign_state "Sovereign state") and fully [independent state](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Independent_state "Independent state"). He moved to end his country's traditional isolation by establishing diplomatic relations with the international community, particularly with the [Soviet Union](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union "Soviet Union") and the [Weimar Republic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Weimar_Republic "Weimar Republic").[^115] [^116] He proclaimed himself [King of Afghanistan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/King_of_Afghanistan "King of Afghanistan") on 9 June 1926, forming the [Kingdom of Afghanistan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kingdom_of_Afghanistan "Kingdom of Afghanistan"). He introduced several reforms intended to modernize his nation. A key force behind these reforms was [Mahmud Tarzi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mahmud_Tarzi "Mahmud Tarzi"), an ardent supporter of the education of women. He fought for Article 68 of Afghanistan's [1923 constitution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1923_Constitution_of_Afghanistan "1923 Constitution of Afghanistan"), which implemented compulsory elementary education. [Slavery](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Slavery "Slavery") was abolished in 1923.[^117] King Amanullah's wife, [Queen Soraya](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soraya_Tarzi "Soraya Tarzi"), was an important figure during this period in the fight for women's education and against their oppression.[^118]

Some of the reforms, such as the abolition of the traditional [burqa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Burqa "Burqa") for women and the opening of co-educational schools, alienated many tribal and religious leaders, leading to the [Afghan Civil War (1928–1929)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afghan_Civil_War_\(1928%E2%80%931929\) "Afghan Civil War (1928–1929)"). King Amanullah abdicated in January 1929, and soon after Kabul fell to [Saqqawist](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saqqawists "Saqqawists") forces led by [Habibullah Kalakani](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Habibullah_Kalakani "Habibullah Kalakani").[^119] [Mohammad Nadir Shah](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mohammad_Nadir_Shah "Mohammad Nadir Shah"), Amanullah's cousin, defeated and killed Kalakani in October 1929, and was declared King Nadir Shah.[^120] He abandoned the reforms of King Amanullah in favor of a more gradual approach to modernization, but was assassinated in 1933 by [Abdul Khaliq](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abdul_Khaliq_Hazara_\(assassin\) "Abdul Khaliq Hazara (assassin)").[^121]

Mohammed Zahir Shah succeeded to the throne and reigned as king from 1933 to 1973. During the [tribal revolts of 1944–1947](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afghan_tribal_revolts_of_1944%E2%80%931947 "Afghan tribal revolts of 1944–1947"), King Zahir's reign was challenged by [Zadran](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zadran_\(Pashtun_tribe\) "Zadran (Pashtun tribe)"), [Safi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Safi_\(tribe\) "Safi (tribe)"), [Mangal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mangal_\(Pashtun_tribe\) "Mangal (Pashtun tribe)"), and [Wazir](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wazir_\(Pashtun_tribe\) "Wazir (Pashtun tribe)") tribesmen led by [Mazrak Zadran](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mazrak_Zadran "Mazrak Zadran"), [Salemai](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Salemai "Salemai"), and [Faqir of Ipi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Faqir_of_Ipi "Faqir of Ipi"), among others – many of whom were [Amanullah loyalists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amanullah_loyalism "Amanullah loyalism"). Afghanistan joined the [League of Nations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/League_of_Nations "League of Nations") in 1934. In the 1930s, the country experienced significant development of roads, infrastructure, the founding of a [national bank](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Da_Afghanistan_Bank "Da Afghanistan Bank"), and increased education. Road links in the north played a large part in a growing cotton and textile industry.[^122] The country built close relationships with the [Axis powers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Axis_powers "Axis powers"), with [Nazi Germany](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nazi_Germany "Nazi Germany") having the largest share in Afghan development at the time.[^123]

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Portrait_of_King_Zahir_Shah_of_Afghanistan_in_1963.jpg/250px-Portrait_of_King_Zahir_Shah_of_Afghanistan_in_1963.jpg)

King Zahir, the last reigning monarch of Afghanistan, who reigned from 1933 until 1973

Until 1946, King Zahir ruled with the assistance of his uncle, who held the post of [prime minister](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Afghanistan "Prime Minister of Afghanistan") and continued the policies of Nadir Shah. Another uncle, [Shah Mahmud Khan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shah_Mahmud_Khan "Shah Mahmud Khan"), became prime minister in 1946 and experimented with allowing greater political freedom. He was replaced in 1953 by [Mohammed Daoud Khan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mohammed_Daoud_Khan "Mohammed Daoud Khan"), a Pashtun nationalist who sought the creation of a [Pashtunistan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pashtunistan "Pashtunistan"), leading to highly tense relations with Pakistan.[^124] Daoud Khan pressed for social modernization reforms and sought a closer relationship with the Soviet Union. Afterward, the [1964 constitution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1964_Constitution_of_Afghanistan "1964 Constitution of Afghanistan") was formed, and the first non-royal prime minister was sworn in.[^122]

Zahir Shah, like his father Nadir Shah, had a policy of maintaining national independence while pursuing gradual modernization, creating nationalist feeling, and improving relations with the United Kingdom. Afghanistan was neither a participant in [World War II](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_War_II "World War II") nor aligned with either power bloc in the [Cold War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War "Cold War"). However, it was a beneficiary of the latter rivalry as both the Soviet Union and the United States vied for influence by building Afghanistan's main highways, airports, and other vital infrastructure. On a per capita basis, Afghanistan received more Soviet [development aid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Development_aid "Development aid") than any other country. In 1973, while the King was in Italy, Daoud Khan launched a [bloodless coup](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1973_Afghan_coup "1973 Afghan coup") and became the first [president of Afghanistan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/President_of_Afghanistan "President of Afghanistan"), abolishing the monarchy.

### Democratic Republic and Soviet War

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Afgan1987_Gardez_UAZ469.jpg/250px-Afgan1987_Gardez_UAZ469.jpg)

Soviet troops in Gardez, Afghanistan, in 1987

In April 1978, the communist [People's Democratic Party of Afghanistan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/People%27s_Democratic_Party_of_Afghanistan "People's Democratic Party of Afghanistan") (PDPA) seized power in a bloody coup d'état against then-President [Mohammed Daoud Khan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mohammed_Daoud_Khan "Mohammed Daoud Khan"), in what is called the [Saur Revolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saur_Revolution "Saur Revolution"). The PDPA declared the establishment of the [Democratic Republic of Afghanistan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_Afghanistan "Democratic Republic of Afghanistan"), with its first leader named as [People's Democratic Party](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/People%27s_Democratic_Party_of_Afghanistan "People's Democratic Party of Afghanistan") General Secretary [Nur Muhammad Taraki](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nur_Muhammad_Taraki "Nur Muhammad Taraki").[^125] This would trigger a series of events that would dramatically turn Afghanistan from a peaceful (albeit poor and secluded) country to a hotbed of conflict and terrorism.[^126] The PDPA initiated various social, symbolic, and land distribution reforms that provoked strong opposition, while also brutally oppressing political dissidents. This caused unrest and quickly expanded into a state of [civil war](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afghan_conflict "Afghan conflict") by 1979, waged by guerrilla *[mujahideen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mujahideen "Mujahideen")* (and smaller [Maoist](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maoist "Maoist") guerrillas) against regime forces countrywide. It quickly turned into a [proxy war](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proxy_war "Proxy war") as the Pakistani government provided these rebels with covert training centers, the United States [supported](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Operation_Cyclone "Operation Cyclone") them through Pakistan's [Inter-Services Intelligence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inter-Services_Intelligence "Inter-Services Intelligence") (ISI),[^127] and the Soviet Union sent thousands of military advisers to support the PDPA regime.[^128] Meanwhile, there was increasingly hostile friction between the competing factions of the PDPA – the dominant [Khalq](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Khalq "Khalq") and the more moderate [Parcham](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parcham "Parcham").[^129]

In October 1979, PDPA General Secretary Taraki was assassinated in an internal coup orchestrated by then-prime minister [Hafizullah Amin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hafizullah_Amin "Hafizullah Amin"), who became the new general secretary of the PDPA. The situation in the country deteriorated under Amin, and thousands of people went missing.[^130] Displeased with Amin's government, the [Soviet Army](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Armed_Forces "Soviet Armed Forces") invaded the country in December 1979, heading for Kabul and killing Amin.[^131] A Soviet-organized regime, led by Parcham's [Babrak Karmal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Babrak_Karmal "Babrak Karmal") but inclusive of both factions (Parcham and Khalq), filled the vacuum. Soviet troops in more substantial numbers were deployed to stabilize Afghanistan under Karmal, marking the beginning of the [Soviet–Afghan War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet%E2%80%93Afghan_War "Soviet–Afghan War").[^132] Lasting nine years, the war caused the deaths of between 562,000 [^133] and 2 million Afghans,[^9] and displaced about 6 million people who subsequently fled Afghanistan, mainly to Pakistan and Iran.[^141] Heavy air bombardment destroyed many countryside villages, millions of [landmines](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Landmine "Landmine") were planted,[^142] and some cities such as Herat and Kandahar were also damaged from bombardment. After the [Soviet withdrawal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_withdrawal_from_Afghanistan "Soviet withdrawal from Afghanistan"), another [civil war ensued](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afghan_Civil_War_\(1989-92\) "Afghan Civil War (1989-92)") until the communist regime under PDPA leader [Mohammad Najibullah](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mohammad_Najibullah "Mohammad Najibullah") collapsed in 1992.[^143] [^144] [^145]

The Soviet–Afghan War had drastic social effects on Afghanistan. The militarization of society led to heavily armed police, private bodyguards, openly armed civil defense groups, and the like becoming the norm in Afghanistan for decades thereafter.[^146] The traditional power structure had shifted from the clergy, community elders, intelligentsia, and military in favor of powerful [warlords](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Warlords "Warlords").[^147]

### Post–Cold War conflict

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/War_in_Afghanistan_%281992%E2%80%932001%29.png/500px-War_in_Afghanistan_%281992%E2%80%932001%29.png)

Development of the civil war from 1992 to late 2001

Another civil war broke out after the [creation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peshawar_Accords "Peshawar Accords") of a dysfunctional coalition [government](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Islamic_State_of_Afghanistan "Islamic State of Afghanistan") between leaders of various *mujahideen* factions. Amid a state of anarchy and factional infighting,[^148] [^149] [^150] various *mujahideen* factions committed widespread rape, murder, and extortion,[^149] [^151] [^152] while Kabul was heavily bombarded and partially destroyed by the fighting.[^152] Several failed reconciliations and alliances occurred between different leaders.[^153] The [Taliban](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taliban "Taliban") emerged in September 1994 as a movement and militia of students (*talib*) from Islamic [madrassas (schools) in Pakistan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Madrassas_in_Pakistan "Madrassas in Pakistan"),[^152] [^154] who soon had military support from Pakistan.[^155]

Taking control of Kandahar city that year,[^152] they conquered more territories until finally driving out the government of [Rabbani](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Burhanuddin_Rabbani "Burhanuddin Rabbani") from Kabul in 1996,[^156] [^157] where they established an [emirate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Islamic_Emirate_of_Afghanistan_\(1996%E2%80%932001\) "Islamic Emirate of Afghanistan (1996–2001)").[^158] The Taliban were condemned internationally for the harsh enforcement of their interpretation of Islamic [sharia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sharia "Sharia") law, which resulted in the brutal treatment of many Afghans, especially [women](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taliban_treatment_of_women "Taliban treatment of women").[^159] [^160] During their rule, the Taliban and their allies committed massacres against Afghan civilians, denied UN food supplies to starving civilians, and conducted a policy of [scorched earth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scorched_earth "Scorched earth"), burning vast areas of fertile land and destroying tens of thousands of homes.[^10]

[After the fall of Kabul](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Battle_of_Kabul_\(1992%E2%80%931996\) "Battle of Kabul (1992–1996)") to the Taliban, [Ahmad Shah Massoud](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ahmad_Shah_Massoud "Ahmad Shah Massoud") and [Abdul Rashid Dostum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abdul_Rashid_Dostum "Abdul Rashid Dostum") formed the [Northern Alliance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Northern_Alliance "Northern Alliance"), later joined by others, to resist the Taliban. Dostum's forces were defeated by the Taliban during the [Battles of Mazar-i-Sharif](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Battles_of_Mazar-i-Sharif_\(1997%E2%80%931998\) "Battles of Mazar-i-Sharif (1997–1998)") in 1997 and 1998; Pakistan's Chief of Army Staff, [Pervez Musharraf](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pervez_Musharraf "Pervez Musharraf"), began sending thousands of Pakistani militants to help the Taliban defeat the Northern Alliance.[^11] By 2000, the Northern Alliance only controlled 10% of the territory, cornered in the northeast. On 9 September 2001, Massoud was assassinated by two Arab [suicide attackers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suicide_attack "Suicide attack") in [Panjshir Valley](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Panjshir_Valley "Panjshir Valley"). Around 400,000 Afghans died in internal conflicts between 1990 and 2001.[^171]

### US invasion and the Islamic Republic

In October 2001, the [United States invaded Afghanistan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_invasion_of_Afghanistan "United States invasion of Afghanistan") to remove the Taliban from power after they refused to hand over [Osama bin Laden](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Osama_bin_Laden "Osama bin Laden"), the prime suspect of the [September 11 attacks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/September_11_attacks "September 11 attacks"), who was a "guest" of the Taliban and was operating his [al-Qaeda](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Al-Qaeda "Al-Qaeda") network in Afghanistan.[^172] [^173] [^174] The majority of Afghans supported the American invasion.[^175] [^176] During the initial invasion, US and UK forces bombed al-Qaeda training camps, and later, in cooperation with the Northern Alliance, the Taliban regime came to an end.[^177]

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Inbound_Choppers_in_Afghanistan_2008.jpg/250px-Inbound_Choppers_in_Afghanistan_2008.jpg)

US troops and Chinooks in Afghanistan, 2008

In December 2001, after the Taliban government was overthrown, the [Afghan Interim Administration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afghan_Interim_Administration "Afghan Interim Administration") under [Hamid Karzai](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hamid_Karzai "Hamid Karzai") was formed. The [International Security Assistance Force](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Security_Assistance_Force "International Security Assistance Force") (ISAF) was established by the [United Nations Security Council](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council "United Nations Security Council") to assist the [Karzai administration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Presidency_of_Hamid_Karzai "Presidency of Hamid Karzai") and provide basic security.[^178] [^179] By this time, after two decades of war as well as an acute [famine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Famine "Famine") at the time, Afghanistan had one of the highest [infant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Infant_mortality "Infant mortality") and child mortality rates in the world, the lowest life expectancy, much of the population suffered from hunger and malnutrition,[^180] [^181] [^182] and infrastructure was in ruins.[^183] Many foreign donors started providing aid and assistance to rebuild the war-torn country.[^184] [^185] As coalition troops entered Afghanistan to help the [rebuilding process](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reconstruction_in_Afghanistan "Reconstruction in Afghanistan"),[^186] [^187] the [Taliban began an insurgency](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taliban_insurgency "Taliban insurgency") to regain control. Afghanistan remained one of the poorest countries in the world because of a lack of foreign investment, [government corruption](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Corruption_in_Afghanistan "Corruption in Afghanistan"), and the Taliban insurgency.[^188] [^189]

The Afghan government was able to establish some democratic structures, adopting a constitution in 2004 with the name [Islamic Republic of Afghanistan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Islamic_Republic_of_Afghanistan "Islamic Republic of Afghanistan"). Attempts were made, often with the support of foreign donor countries, to improve the country's economy, healthcare, education, transport, and agriculture. ISAF forces also began to train the [Afghan National Security Forces](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afghan_National_Security_Forces "Afghan National Security Forces"). Following 2002, nearly five million [Afghans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afghan_diaspora "Afghan diaspora") were repatriated.[^190] The number of NATO troops present in Afghanistan peaked at 140,000 in 2011,[^191] dropping to approximately 16,000 in 2018.[^192] In September 2014, [Ashraf Ghani](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ashraf_Ghani "Ashraf Ghani") became president after the [2014 Afghan presidential election](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2014_Afghan_presidential_election "2014 Afghan presidential election"), in which, for the first time in Afghanistan's history, power was democratically transferred.[^193] [^194] [^195] On 28 December 2014, NATO formally ended ISAF combat operations and transferred full security responsibility to the Afghan government. The NATO-led [Operation Resolute Support](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Resolute_Support_Mission "Resolute Support Mission") was formed the same day as a successor to ISAF.[^196] [^197] Thousands of NATO troops remained in the country to train and advise Afghan government forces [^198] and continue their fight against the Taliban.[^199] A report titled *Body Count* concluded that 106,000–170,000 civilians had been killed as a result of the fighting in Afghanistan at the hands of all parties to the conflict.[^200]

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/2021_Taliban_Offensive.png/250px-2021_Taliban_Offensive.png)

A map of Afghanistan showing the 2021 Taliban offensive

On 19 February 2020, the [United States–Taliban deal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States%E2%80%93Taliban_deal "United States–Taliban deal") was made in Qatar. The deal was one of the critical events that caused the collapse of the Afghan National Security Forces (ANSF);[^201] following the signing of the deal, the US dramatically reduced the number of air attacks and deprived the ANSF of a critical edge in fighting the [Taliban insurgency](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taliban_insurgency "Taliban insurgency"), leading to the Taliban takeover of Kabul.[^202]

### Second Taliban era

NATO Secretary General [Jens Stoltenberg](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jens_Stoltenberg "Jens Stoltenberg") announced on 14 April 2021 that the alliance had agreed to start [withdrawing its troops from Afghanistan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2020%E2%80%932021_US_troop_withdrawal_from_Afghanistan "2020–2021 US troop withdrawal from Afghanistan") by 1 May.[^203] Soon after NATO troops began withdrawing, the Taliban launched an [offensive against the Afghan government](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2021_Taliban_offensive "2021 Taliban offensive") and quickly advanced in front of collapsing Afghan government forces.[^204] [^205] The Taliban [captured the capital city of Kabul](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fall_of_Kabul_\(2021\) "Fall of Kabul (2021)") on 15 August 2021, after regaining control over a vast majority of Afghanistan.[^206] Several foreign diplomats and Afghan government officials, including President Ashraf Ghani,[^207] were evacuated from the country, with many Afghan civilians attempting to flee along with them.[^208] On 17 August, first vice president [Amrullah Saleh](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amrullah_Saleh "Amrullah Saleh") proclaimed himself caretaker president and announced the formation of an [anti-Taliban front](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Resistance_Front_of_Afghanistan "National Resistance Front of Afghanistan") with a reported 6,000+ troops [^209] [^210] in the [Panjshir Valley](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Panjshir_Valley "Panjshir Valley"), along with [Ahmad Massoud](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ahmad_Massoud "Ahmad Massoud").[^211] [^212] However, by 6 September, the Taliban had taken control of most of [Panjshir Province](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Panjshir_Province "Panjshir Province"), with resistance fighters retreating to the mountains.[^213] Clashes in the valley ceased in mid-September.[^214]

According to the [Costs of War Project](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Costs_of_War_Project "Costs of War Project"), 176,000 people were killed in the conflict, including 46,319 civilians, between 2001 and 2021.[^215] According to the [Uppsala Conflict Data Program](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Uppsala_Conflict_Data_Program "Uppsala Conflict Data Program"), at least 212,191 people were killed in the conflict.[^216] Though the state of war in the country ended in 2021, armed conflict persists in some regions [^217] [^218] [^219] amid [fighting between the Taliban and the local branch of the Islamic State](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Islamic_State%E2%80%93Taliban_conflict "Islamic State–Taliban conflict"), as well as an anti-Taliban [Republican insurgency](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Republican_insurgency_in_Afghanistan "Republican insurgency in Afghanistan").[^220]

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Taliban_Humvee_in_Kabul%2C_August_2021_%28cropped%29.png/250px-Taliban_Humvee_in_Kabul%2C_August_2021_%28cropped%29.png)

Taliban fighters in Kabul on a captured Humvee following the 2021 fall of Kabul

The Taliban government is led by [supreme leader](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Head_of_the_Islamic_Emirate_of_Afghanistan "Head of the Islamic Emirate of Afghanistan") [Hibatullah Akhundzada](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hibatullah_Akhundzada "Hibatullah Akhundzada") [^221] and acting prime minister [Hasan Akhund](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hasan_Akhund "Hasan Akhund"), who took office on 7 September 2021.[^222] [^223] Akhund is one of the four founders of the Taliban [^224] and was a deputy prime minister of the previous emirate; his appointment was seen as a compromise between moderates and hardliners.[^225] A [new, all-male cabinet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cabinet_of_Afghanistan "Cabinet of Afghanistan") was formed, which included [Abdul Hakim Haqqani](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abdul_Hakim_Haqqani "Abdul Hakim Haqqani") as minister of justice.[^226] [^227] On 20 September 2021, [United Nations Secretary-General](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Secretary-General_of_the_United_Nations "Secretary-General of the United Nations") [António Guterres](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ant%C3%B3nio_Guterres "António Guterres") received a letter from acting [minister of foreign affairs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ministry_of_Foreign_Affairs_\(Afghanistan\) "Ministry of Foreign Affairs (Afghanistan)") [Amir Khan Muttaqi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amir_Khan_Muttaqi "Amir Khan Muttaqi") to formally claim Afghanistan's seat as a member state for their official spokesman in [Doha](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Doha "Doha"), [Suhail Shaheen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suhail_Shaheen "Suhail Shaheen"). The United Nations did not recognize the previous Taliban government and chose to work with the then government-in-exile instead.[^228]

Western nations suspended most of their humanitarian aid to Afghanistan following the Taliban's August 2021 takeover of the country; the [World Bank](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_Bank "World Bank") and [International Monetary Fund](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund "International Monetary Fund") also halted their payments.[^229] [^230] More than half of Afghanistan's 39 million people faced an acute [food shortage](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Food_security "Food security") in October 2021.[^231] Human Rights Watch reported on 11 November 2021 that Afghanistan was facing widespread [famine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Famine "Famine") due to an economic and banking crisis.[^232]

The Taliban initially significantly tackled corruption, improving on the [corruption perceptions index](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Corruption_Perceptions_Index "Corruption Perceptions Index") from 174th to 150th out of 180 countries from 2021 to 2022,[^233] but dropping to 162nd in 2023.[^234] The Taliban have also reportedly reduced bribery and extortion in public service areas.[^233]

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Iran_vs_Afghanistan.jpg/250px-Iran_vs_Afghanistan.jpg)

A Taliban official with Iranian Foreign Minister Hossein Amir-Abdollahian in 2023

At the same time, the human rights situation in the country has deteriorated.[^235] Following the 2001 invasion, more than 5.7 million refugees returned to Afghanistan;[^236] however, in 2021, 2.6 million Afghans remained refugees, primarily in Iran and Pakistan, and another 4 million were internally displaced.[^237]

In October 2023, the Pakistani government ordered the [expulsion of Afghans from Pakistan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Expulsion_of_Afghans_from_Pakistan "Expulsion of Afghans from Pakistan").[^238] Iran also decided to deport [Afghan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afghans_in_Iran "Afghans in Iran") nationals back to Afghanistan.[^239] Taliban authorities condemned the deportations of Afghans as an "inhuman act".[^240] Afghanistan faced a humanitarian crisis in late 2023.[^241]

On 10 November 2024, Afghanistan's Foreign Ministry confirmed that Taliban representatives would attend the [2024 United Nations Climate Change Conference](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2024_United_Nations_Climate_Change_Conference "2024 United Nations Climate Change Conference"), marking the first time the country participated since the Taliban's return to power in 2021. Afghanistan had been barred from previous summits due to the lack of global recognition of the Taliban regime.[^242] In May 2025, Iran ordered the [mass deportation of an estimated 4 million Afghan migrants and refugees](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2025_Afghan_deportation_from_Iran "2025 Afghan deportation from Iran").[^243]

The [2025–2026 hunger crisis in Afghanistan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2025%E2%80%932026_hunger_crisis_in_Afghanistan "2025–2026 hunger crisis in Afghanistan") is one of the world's most severe, with over 17 million people—one-third of the population—requiring urgent food assistance. Driven by persistent [drought](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Drought_in_Afghanistan "Drought in Afghanistan"), economic collapse, and massive refugee returns, the crisis has left over 4.9 million children and women needing malnutrition treatment.[^244]

## Geography

Afghanistan is located in Southern-Central Asia.[^245] [^246] [^247] [^248] [^249] The region centered in Afghanistan is considered the "crossroads of Asia",[^250] and the country has had the nickname Heart of Asia.[^251] The renowned [Urdu](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Urdu "Urdu") poet [Allama Iqbal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Allama_Iqbal "Allama Iqbal") once wrote about the country:

> Asia is a body of water and earth, of which the Afghan nation is the heart. From its discord, the discord of Asia; and from its accord, the accord of Asia.

At over 652,864 km <sup>2</sup> (252,072 sq mi),[^252] Afghanistan is the world's [41st largest country](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_countries_and_dependencies_by_area "List of countries and dependencies by area").[^253] It is slightly bigger than France and smaller than Myanmar, and about the size of Texas in the United States. There is no coastline, as Afghanistan is [landlocked](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Landlocked "Landlocked"). Afghanistan shares its longest land border (the [Durand Line](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Durand_Line "Durand Line")) with Pakistan to the east and south, followed by borders with Tajikistan to the northeast, Iran to the west, Turkmenistan to the north-west, Uzbekistan to the north, and China to the far northeast; India claims a border with Afghanistan through Pakistani-administered [Kashmir](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kashmir "Kashmir").[^254] Clockwise from south-west, Afghanistan shares borders with the [Sistan and Baluchestan Province](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sistan_and_Baluchestan_Province "Sistan and Baluchestan Province"), South Khorasan Province, and [Razavi Khorasan Province](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Razavi_Khorasan_Province "Razavi Khorasan Province") of Iran; [Ahal Region](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ahal_Region "Ahal Region"), [Mary Region](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mary_Region "Mary Region"), and [Lebap Region](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lebap_Region "Lebap Region") of Turkmenistan; [Surxondaryo Region](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Surxondaryo_Region "Surxondaryo Region") of Uzbekistan; [Khatlon Region](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Khatlon_Region "Khatlon Region") and [Gorno-Badakhshan Autonomous Region](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gorno-Badakhshan "Gorno-Badakhshan") of Tajikistan; [Xinjiang Uyghur Autonomous Region](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Xinjiang "Xinjiang") of China; and the [Gilgit-Baltistan territory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gilgit-Baltistan "Gilgit-Baltistan"), [Khyber Pakhtunkhwa province](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Khyber_Pakhtunkhwa "Khyber Pakhtunkhwa"), and [Balochistan province](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Balochistan,_Pakistan "Balochistan, Pakistan") of Pakistan.[^255]

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/FrontLines_Environment_Photo_Contest_Winner_-5_%285808476109%29.jpg/250px-FrontLines_Environment_Photo_Contest_Winner_-5_%285808476109%29.jpg)

Floodplain cultivation in the Wakhan Corridor, Pamir Mountains

The geography in Afghanistan is varied, but is mostly mountainous and rugged, with some unusual mountain ridges accompanied by plateaus and river basins.[^256] It is dominated by the [Hindu Kush](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hindu_Kush "Hindu Kush") range, the western extension of the [Himalayas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Himalayas "Himalayas") that stretches to eastern [Tibet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tibet "Tibet") via the [Pamir Mountains](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pamir_Mountains "Pamir Mountains") and [Karakoram](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karakoram "Karakoram") Mountains in Afghanistan's far north-east. Most of the highest points are in the east, consisting of fertile mountain valleys, often considered part of the " [Roof of the World](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roof_of_the_World "Roof of the World") ". The Hindu Kush ends at the west-central highlands, creating plains in the north and southwest, namely the [Turkestan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Turkestan "Turkestan") Plains and the [Sistan Basin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sistan_Basin "Sistan Basin"); these two regions consist of rolling grasslands and semi-deserts, as well as hot, windy deserts, respectively.[^257] Forests exist in the corridor between the provinces of [Nuristan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nuristan_Province "Nuristan Province") and [Paktika](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paktika_Province "Paktika Province") (see [East Afghan montane conifer forests](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/East_Afghan_montane_conifer_forests "East Afghan montane conifer forests")),[^258] and [tundra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tundra "Tundra") in the northeast. The country's highest point is [Noshaq](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Noshaq "Noshaq"), at 7,492 m (24,580 ft) above sea level.[^259] The lowest point is located in [Jowzjan Province](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jowzjan_Province "Jowzjan Province") along the Amu River bank, at 258 m (846 ft) above sea level.

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Afghan_topo_en.jpg/500px-Afghan_topo_en.jpg)

The mountainous topography of Afghanistan

Despite having numerous rivers and [reservoirs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_dams_and_reservoirs_in_Afghanistan "List of dams and reservoirs in Afghanistan"), large parts of the country are dry. The [endorheic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Endorheic "Endorheic") Sistan Basin is one of the world's driest regions.[^260] The [Amu Darya](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amu_Darya "Amu Darya") rises in the north of the Hindu Kush, while the nearby [Hari River](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hari_\(Afghanistan\) "Hari (Afghanistan)") flows west towards [Herat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Herat "Herat"), and the [Arghandab River](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arghandab_River "Arghandab River") flows from the central region southwards. To the south and west of the Hindu Kush flow a number of streams that are tributaries of the [Indus River](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indus_River "Indus River"),[^256] such as the [Helmand River](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Helmand_River "Helmand River"). The [Kabul River](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kabul_River "Kabul River") flows in an easterly direction to the Indus, ending at the Indian Ocean.[^261] Afghanistan receives heavy snow during the winter in the Hindu Kush and Pamir Mountains, and the melting snow in the spring season enters the [rivers, lakes, and streams](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_rivers_of_Afghanistan "List of rivers of Afghanistan").[^262] [^263] However, two-thirds of the country's water flows into the neighboring countries of Iran, Pakistan, and [Turkmenistan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Turkmenistan "Turkmenistan"). As reported in 2010, the state needs more than US$2 billion to rehabilitate its irrigation systems so that the water is properly managed.[^264]

In Afghanistan, [forest cover](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Forest_cover "Forest cover") accounts for approximately 2% of the total land area, equivalent to 1,208,440 hectares (ha) of forest in 2020, which remained unchanged since 1990. Of the naturally regenerating forest, 0% was reported to be [primary forest](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Primary_forest "Primary forest") (consisting of native tree species with no clearly visible indications of human activity), and around 0% of the forest area was found within protected areas. For the year 2015, 100% of the forest area was reported to be under [public ownership](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/State_ownership "State ownership").[^265] [^266]

The northeastern Hindu Kush [mountain range](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mountain_range "Mountain range"), located in and around the [Badakhshan Province](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Badakhshan_Province "Badakhshan Province") of Afghanistan, is situated in a [geologically active](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Natural_environment#Geological_activity "Natural environment") area where earthquakes occur almost every year.[^267] They can be deadly and destructive, causing [landslides](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Landslide "Landslide") in some parts or [avalanches](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2009_Afghan_avalanches "2009 Afghan avalanches") during the winter.[^268] In June 2022, a [destructive 5.9 earthquake](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/June_2022_Afghanistan_earthquake "June 2022 Afghanistan earthquake") struck near the border with Pakistan, killing at least 1,150 people and sparking fears of a major humanitarian crisis.[^269] On 7 October 2023, a [6.3 magnitude earthquake](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/October_2023_Afghanistan_earthquake "October 2023 Afghanistan earthquake") struck northwest of Herat, killing over 1,400 people.[^270]

### Climate

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Koppen-Geiger_Map_v2_AFG_1991%E2%80%932020.svg/250px-Koppen-Geiger_Map_v2_AFG_1991%E2%80%932020.svg.png)

Köppen climate map of Afghanistan 271

Afghanistan has a [continental climate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Continental_climate "Continental climate") with harsh winters in the [central highlands](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hazarajat "Hazarajat"), the glaciated northeast (around [Nuristan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nuristan "Nuristan")), and the [Wakhan Corridor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wakhan_Corridor "Wakhan Corridor"), where the average temperature in January is below −15 °C (5 °F) and can reach −26 °C (−15 °F),[^256] and hot summers in the low-lying areas of the [Sistan Basin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sistan_Basin "Sistan Basin") of the southwest, the [Jalalabad](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jalalabad "Jalalabad") basin in the east, and the [Turkestan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afghan_Turkestan "Afghan Turkestan") plains along the [Amu River](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amu_River "Amu River") in the north, where temperatures average over 35 °C (95 °F) in July [^259] [^272] and can go over 43 °C (109 °F).[^256] The country is generally [arid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arid "Arid") in the summers, with most rainfall falling between December and April. The lower areas of northern and western Afghanistan are the driest, with precipitation more common in the east. Although proximate to India, Afghanistan is mostly outside the [monsoon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Monsoon "Monsoon") zone,[^256] except the [Nuristan Province](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nuristan_Province "Nuristan Province"), which occasionally receives summer monsoon rain.[^273]

Although Afghanistan has contributed minimally to global [greenhouse gas emissions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greenhouse_gas_emissions "Greenhouse gas emissions"), it is one of the most vulnerable countries to [climate change](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change "Climate change") and least prepared to cope with its impacts.[^274] [Climate change in Afghanistan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_change_in_Afghanistan "Climate change in Afghanistan") is causing more frequent and severe droughts.[^275] [^276] Severe drought conditions affect 25 of the country's 34 provinces, impacting over half the population.[^277] These droughts cause [desertification](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Desertification "Desertification"),[^277] [^278] reduce [food](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Food_security "Food security") [^279] and [water security](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Water_security "Water security"),[^280] disrupt agriculture, and cause [internal displacement](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internally_displaced_person "Internally displaced person").[^281] Extreme rainfall over short periods is also more likely, increasing the risk of [floods](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Flood "Flood") and [landslides](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Landslide "Landslide").[^282] Due to rising temperatures, almost 14% of Afghanistan's glacier coverage was lost between 1990 and 2015,[^283] increasing the risk of [glacial lake outburst floods](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glacial_lake_outburst_flood "Glacial lake outburst flood").[^284] By 2050, climate change could displace an additional 5 million people within Afghanistan.[^281] [^285]

### Biodiversity

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Schneeleopard-_P1020498.jpg/250px-Schneeleopard-_P1020498.jpg)

The snow leopard is the official national animal of Afghanistan.

Several types of mammals exist throughout Afghanistan. [Snow leopards](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Snow_leopard "Snow leopard") and [brown bears](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brown_bear "Brown bear") live in the high elevation [alpine tundra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alpine_tundra "Alpine tundra") regions. The [Marco Polo sheep](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marco_Polo_sheep "Marco Polo sheep") exclusively live in the Wakhan Corridor region of north-east Afghanistan. Foxes, [wolves](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wolves "Wolves"), [otters](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Otter "Otter"), [deer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deer "Deer"), [wild sheep](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wild_sheep "Wild sheep"), [lynx](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lynx "Lynx") and other big cats populate the mountain forest region of the east. In the semi-desert northern plains, wildlife include a variety of birds, [hedgehogs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hedgehog "Hedgehog"), [gophers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gopher "Gopher"), and large carnivores such as [jackals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Golden_jackal "Golden jackal") and [hyenas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Striped_hyena "Striped hyena").[^286]

[Gazelles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gazelle "Gazelle"), [wild pigs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wild_boar "Wild boar") and jackals populate the [steppe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Steppe "Steppe") plains of the south and west, while [mongoose](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mongoose "Mongoose") and cheetahs exist in the semi-desert south.[^286] [Marmots](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marmot "Marmot") and [ibex](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ibex "Ibex") also live in the high mountains of Afghanistan, and [pheasants](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pheasant "Pheasant") exist in some parts of the country.[^287] The [Afghan hound](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afghan_hound "Afghan hound") is a native breed of dog known for its fast speed and its long hair; it is relatively known in the west.[^288]
