[Endemic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Endemic "Endemic") fauna of Afghanistan includes the [Afghan flying squirrel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afghan_flying_squirrel "Afghan flying squirrel"), [Afghan snowfinch](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afghan_snowfinch "Afghan snowfinch"), *[Paradactylodon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paradactylodon "Paradactylodon")* (or the " [Paghman](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paghman "Paghman") mountain salamander"), *[Stigmella kasyi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stigmella_kasyi "Stigmella kasyi")*, *[Vulcaniella kabulensis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vulcaniella_kabulensis "Vulcaniella kabulensis")*, [Afghan leopard gecko](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afghan_leopard_gecko "Afghan leopard gecko"), *[Wheeleria parviflorellus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wheeleria_parviflorellus "Wheeleria parviflorellus")*, among others. Endemic flora include *[Iris afghanica](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iris_afghanica "Iris afghanica")*. Afghanistan has a wide variety of birds despite its relatively arid climate – an estimated 460 species of which 235 breed within.[^288]

The forest region of Afghanistan has vegetation such as [pine trees](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pine_tree "Pine tree"), [spruce trees](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spruce_tree "Spruce tree"), [fir trees](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fir_tree "Fir tree") and [larches](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Larch "Larch"), whereas the steppe grassland regions consist of [broadleaf trees](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Broadleaf_tree "Broadleaf tree"), short grass, [perennial plants](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Perennial_plant "Perennial plant") and [shrublands](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shrubland "Shrubland"). The colder high elevation regions are composed of hardy grasses and small flowering plants.[^286] Several regions are designated [protected areas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_protected_areas_of_Afghanistan "List of protected areas of Afghanistan"); there are three [national parks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_parks "National parks"): [Band-e Amir](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Band-e_Amir "Band-e Amir"), [Wakhan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wakhan_National_Park "Wakhan National Park") and [Nuristan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nuristan_National_Park "Nuristan National Park"). Afghanistan had a 2018 [Forest Landscape Integrity Index](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Forest_Landscape_Integrity_Index "Forest Landscape Integrity Index") mean score of 8.85/10, ranking it 15th globally out of 172 countries.[^289]

## Government and politics

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/200229-D-AP390-1529_%2849603221753%29.jpg/250px-200229-D-AP390-1529_%2849603221753%29.jpg)

The Arg (the Presidential palace) in Kabul, photographed in 2020

Following the effective collapse of the [Islamic Republic of Afghanistan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Islamic_Republic_of_Afghanistan "Islamic Republic of Afghanistan") during the [2021 Taliban offensive](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2021_Taliban_offensive "2021 Taliban offensive"), the Taliban declared the country an Islamic Emirate. A new caretaker government was announced on 7 September.[^290] As of July 2025, only the [Russian Federation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russian_Federation "Russian Federation") has [recognized the Islamic Emirate of Afghanistan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Recognition_of_the_Islamic_Emirate_of_Afghanistan "Recognition of the Islamic Emirate of Afghanistan") as the *[de jure](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/De_jure "De jure")* government of Afghanistan.[^291] In the [V-Dem Democracy Indices](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/V-Dem_Democracy_Indices "V-Dem Democracy Indices"), Afghanistan ranks amongst the lowest as a "closed autocracy," ranking at 176th and 177th out of 179 countries in electoral democracy and liberal democracy indices respectively.[^292] Afghanistan operates on a centralised [unitary form of government](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Unitary_state "Unitary state").[^293]

A traditional instrument of governance in Afghanistan is the *[loya jirga](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Loya_jirga "Loya jirga")* (grand assembly), a Pashtun consultative meeting that was mainly organized for choosing a new [head of state](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Head_of_state "Head of state"), adopting a new constitution, or to settle national or regional issue such as war.[^294] Loya jirgas have been held since at least 1747,[^295] with [the most recent one](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afghanistan_Ulema_gathering "Afghanistan Ulema gathering") occurring from June to July 2022.[^296] [^297] [^298]

[^1]: The last census in Afghanistan was conducted in 1979, and was itself incomplete. Due to the [ongoing conflict](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afghan_conflict "Afghan conflict") in the country, no official census has been conducted since.[^16]

[^2]: Other demonyms that have been used are Afghani,[^22] Afghanese and Afghanistani (see [Afghans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afghans "Afghans") for further details) [^12]

[^3]: Afghanistan is a pure [autocracy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Autocracy "Autocracy"), with all law ultimately originating from the supreme leader.[^28] [^29] There is an advisory [Leadership Council](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leadership_Council_of_Afghanistan "Leadership Council of Afghanistan"), which is not a legislature as it has no power to pass laws.

[^4]: The last census was conducted in 1979. Sources disagree about the current population:

- The Afghani [National Statistics and Information Authority](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Statistics_and_Information_Authority "National Statistics and Information Authority") gives an estimate of 35,695,527 for 2024.[^35] [^36]
- The [Encyclopædia Britannica](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Encyclop%C3%A6dia_Britannica "Encyclopædia Britannica") gives an estimate of 36,432,000 for 2025.[^37]
- The BBC gives a figure of 38.3 million for 2023.[^38]
- The CIA gives an estimate of 40,121,552 for 2024.[^39]
- The UN gives an estimate of 42,045,000 for 2024.[^40] [^41]
- The US Census Bureau provides an estimate of 49,552,566 for 2025.[^42]
All figures are mid-year.

- [Pashto](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pashto_language "Pashto language"): افغانستان, [romanized](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Romanization_of_Pashto "Romanization of Pashto"): *Afğānistān* [\[ʔaʊ.ɣɑ.nis.tɑn/ʔap.ɣɑ.nis.tɑn\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Help:IPA/Pashto "Help:IPA/Pashto")

[^7]: The [Government of India](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Government_of_India "Government of India") regards Afghanistan as a bordering country, as it considers all of [Kashmir](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kashmir "Kashmir") to be part of India. However, this is [disputed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kashmir_conflict "Kashmir conflict"), and the region bordering Afghanistan is administered by Pakistan as [Gilgit-Baltistan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gilgit-Baltistan "Gilgit-Baltistan").[^34]

[^8]: As of July 2025, [Russia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russia "Russia") remains the only UN member state to have extended diplomatic recognition to the Taliban government.[^50]

[^53]: - "The name Afghan has evidently been derived from Asvakan, the Assakenoi of Arrian... " (Megasthenes and Arrian, p 180. See also: Alexander's Invasion of India, p 38; J.W. McCrindle).
- "Even the name Afghan is Aryan being derived from Asvakayana, an important clan of the Asvakas or horsemen who must have derived this title from their handling of celebrated breeds of horses" (See: Imprints of Indian Thought and Culture Abroad, p 124, Vivekananda Kendra Prakashan).
- Cf: "Their name (Afghan) means "cavalier" being derived from the [Sanskrit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sanskrit "Sanskrit"), *Asva*, or *Asvaka*, a horse, and shows that their country must have been noted in ancient times, as it is at the present day, for its superior breed of horses. Asvaka was an important tribe settled north to Kabul river, which offered a gallant resistance but ineffectual resistance to the arms of Alexander." (*Scottish Geographical Magazine*, 1999, p. 275, Royal Scottish Geographical Society)
- "Afghans are Assakani of the [Greeks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greeks "Greeks"); this word being the [Sanskrit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sanskrit "Sanskrit") [Ashvaka](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ashvaka "Ashvaka") meaning 'horsemen'." (*Sva*, 1915, p. 113, Christopher Molesworth Birdwood)
- Cf: "The name represents Sanskrit Asvaka in the sense of a *cavalier*, and this reappears scarcely modified in the Assakani or Assakeni of the historians of the expedition of [Alexander](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_the_Great "Alexander the Great") " (Hobson-Jobson: *A Glossary of Colloquial [Anglo-Indian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anglo-Indian "Anglo-Indian") words and phrases, and of kindred terms, etymological*. Henry Yule, A. D. Burnell).

[^134]: Kakar, Mohammed (3 March 1997). [*The Soviet Invasion and the Afghan Response, 1979–1982*](http://publishing.cdlib.org/ucpressebooks/view?docId=ft7b69p12h;brand=ucpress). University of California Press. [ISBN](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ISBN_\(identifier\) "ISBN (identifier)") [978-0-520-20893-3](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-520-20893-3 "Special:BookSources/978-0-520-20893-3"). [Archived](https://web.archive.org/web/20170106175142/http://publishing.cdlib.org/ucpressebooks/view?docId=ft7b69p12h;brand=ucpress) from the original on 6 January 2017. Retrieved 7 January 2017. The Afghans are among the latest victims of genocide by a superpower. Large numbers of Afghans were killed to suppress resistance to the army of the Soviet Union, which wished to vindicate its client regime and realize its goal in Afghanistan.

[^135]: Klass, Rosanne (1994). [*The Widening Circle of Genocide*](https://books.google.com/books?id=I2chrSJCW54C&pg=PA129). Transaction Publishers. p. 129. [ISBN](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ISBN_\(identifier\) "ISBN (identifier)") [978-1-4128-3965-5](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-4128-3965-5 "Special:BookSources/978-1-4128-3965-5"). During the intervening fourteen years of Communist rule, an estimated 1.5 to 2 million Afghan civilians were killed by Soviet forces and their proxies- the four Communist regimes in Kabul, and the East Germans, Bulgarians, Czechs, Cubans, Palestinians, Indians and others who assisted them. These were not battle casualties or the unavoidable civilian victims of warfare. Soviet and local Communist forces seldom attacked the scattered guerilla bands of the Afghan Resistance except, in a few strategic locales like the Panjsher valley. Instead they deliberately targeted the civilian population, primarily in the rural areas.

According to widely reported accounts, substantial programmes of depopulation have been conducted in these Afghan provinces: Ghazni, Nagarhar, Lagham, Qandahar, Zabul, Badakhshan, Lowgar, Paktia, Paktika and Kunar...There is considerable evidence that genocide has been committed against the Afghan people by the combined forces of the Democratic Republic of Afghanistan and the Soviet Union.

[^175]: ["WPO Poll: Afghan Public Overwhelmingly Rejects al-Qaeda, Taliban"](http://drum.lib.umd.edu/bitstream/handle/1903/10127/Afghanistan_Jan06_art2.pdf;jsessionid=51A568EB80A658471A265A1D06EF8ADB?sequence=3). *[University of Maryland Libraries](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_Maryland_Libraries "University of Maryland Libraries")*. 30 January 2006. Retrieved 2 January 2017. Equally large percentages endorse the US military presence in Afghanistan. Eighty-three percent said they have a favorable view of "the US military forces in our country" (39% very favorable). Just 17% have an unfavorable view.

[^176]: ["Afghan Futures: A National Public Opinion Survey"](http://acsor-surveys.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/01/Afghan-Futures-Wave-6-Analysis_FINAL-v2.pdf) (PDF). *[Afghan Center for Socio-economic and Opinion Research](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afghan_Center_for_Socio-economic_and_Opinion_Research "Afghan Center for Socio-economic and Opinion Research")*. 29 January 2015. p. 4. [Archived](https://web.archive.org/web/20170329093740/http://acsor-surveys.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/01/Afghan-Futures-Wave-6-Analysis_FINAL-v2.pdf) (PDF) from the original on 29 March 2017. Retrieved 2 January 2017. Seventy-seven percent support the presence of U.S. forces; 67 percent say the same of NATO/ISAF forces more generally. Despite the country's travails, eight in 10 say it was a good thing for the United States to oust the Taliban in 2001. And much more blame either the Taliban or al Qaeda for the country's violence, 53 percent, than blame the United States, 12 percent. The latter is about half what it was in 2012, coinciding with a sharp reduction in the U.S. deployment.
